# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Rust Edition 2018 to 2021 Import Path Migration

## Your Task

Find all Rust source files in rust-lang/rust (standard library and core) that implement edition-specific import path handling: the edition-based macro_use difference, the prelude v1 vs v2 module exports, and the resolver behavior changes between edition 2018 and 2021.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the migration domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7, sg-evals/servo--be6a2f99.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7` (rust-lang/rust)
- `sg-evals/servo--be6a2f99` (servo/servo)

## Output Format

Create a file at `/workspace/answer.json` with your findings in the following structure:

```json
{

  ],

  ],
  "chain": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "FunctionName"}
  ],

}
```

Include only the fields relevant to this task. Your answer is evaluated against a closed-world oracle — completeness matters.

## Summary

I've identified **42 key files** across the rust-lang/rust codebase (sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7) that implement the three critical aspects of edition-based import path handling:

### Key Findings:

1. **Prelude Module Exports (v1 vs Edition-Versioned)**
   - `library/std/src/prelude/mod.rs` and `library/core/src/prelude/mod.rs` define separate edition modules (rust_2015, rust_2018, rust_2021, rust_2024)
   - Edition 2021 adds `TryFrom`, `TryInto`, and `FromIterator` traits to the prelude
   - Edition 2024 adds `Future` and `IntoFuture`

2. **Edition-Based Macro_use Handling**
   - `compiler/rustc_builtin_macros/src/standard_library_imports.rs` injects edition-specific prelude paths
   - Edition 2015 uses absolute paths (`::std::prelude::rust_2015`), while 2018+ use relative paths
   - MacroUsePrelude is always accessible in 2015 but controlled by implicit_prelude flags in 2018+

3. **Resolver Behavior Changes (2015 vs 2018+)**
   - `compiler/rustc_resolve/src/ident.rs` implements edition-specific scope resolution
   - Edition 2015: `::foo` resolves to crate root with fallback to extern prelude
   - Edition 2018+: `::foo` resolves to extern prelude only (uniform paths)
   - `compiler/rustc_resolve/src/lib.rs` defines `ModuleAndExternPrelude` variant for backward compatibility

4. **2021 Prelude Collisions**
   - `compiler/rustc_hir_typeck/src/method/prelude_edition_lints.rs` detects ambiguous method calls
   - `compiler/rustc_lint_defs/src/builtin.rs` defines RUST_2021_PRELUDE_COLLISIONS lint

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: //! # The Rust Prelude\\n2: //!\\n3: //! Rust comes with a variety of things in its standard library. However, if\\n4: //! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\\n5: //! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\\n6: //! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\\n7: //!\\n8: //! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\\n9: //! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\\n10: //! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\\n11: //! program.\\n12: //!\\n13: //! # Other preludes\\n14: //!\\n15: //! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\\n16: //! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\\n17: //! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\\n18: //! also define their own preludes.\\n19: //!\\n20: //! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\\n21: //!\\n22: //! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\\n23: //! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\\n24: //! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\\n25: //!\\n26: //! # Prelude contents\\n27: //!\\n28: //! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\\n29: //! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\\n30: //! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\\n31: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\\n32: //! It re-exports the following:\\n33: //!\\n34: //! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\\n35: //!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\\n36: //! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\\n37: //!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\\n38: //! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\\n39: //! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\\n40: //!   dropping a value.\\n41: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\\n42: //!   a type or value.\\n43: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\\n44: //!   alignment of a type or value.\\n45: //! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\\n46: //! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\\n47: //!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\\n48: //!   borrowed type.\\n49: //! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\\n50: //!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\\n51: //! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\\n52: //!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\\n53: //!   seen in trait bounds.\\n54: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\\n55: //!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\\n56: //! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\\n57: //! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\\n58: //!   iterators of various\\n59: //!   kinds.\\n60: //! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\\n61: //!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\\n62: //!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\\n63: //! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\\n64: //!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Like [`Option`], its variants are\\n65: //!   exported as well.\\n66: //! * <code>[std::string]::{[String], [ToString]}</code>, heap-allocated strings.\\n67: //! * <code>[std::vec]::[Vec]</code>, a growable, heap-allocated vector.\\n68: //!\\n69: //!     "content": "1: //! # The Rust Prelude\n2: //!\n3: //! Rust comes with a variety of things in its standard library. However, if\n4: //! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\n5: //! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\n6: //! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\n7: //!\n8: //! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\n9: //! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\n10: //! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\n11: //! program.\n12: //!\n13: //! # Other preludes\n14: //!\n15: //! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\n16: //! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\n17: //! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\n18: //! also define their own preludes.\n19: //!\n20: //! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\n21: //!\n22: //! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\n23: //! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\n24: //! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\n25: //!\n26: //! # Prelude contents\n27: //!\n28: //! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\n29: //! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\n30: //! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\n31: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\n32: //! It re-exports the following:\n33: //!\n34: //! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\n35: //!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\n36: //! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\n37: //!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\n38: //! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\n39: //! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\n40: //!   dropping a value.\n41: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\n42: //!   a type or value.\n43: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\n44: //!   alignment of a type or value.\n45: //! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\n46: //! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\n47: //!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\n48: //!   borrowed type.\n49: //! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\n50: //!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\n51: //! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\n52: //!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\n53: //!   seen in trait bounds.\n54: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\n55: //!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\n56: //! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\n57: //! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\n58: //!   iterators of various\n59: //!   kinds.\n60: //! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\n61: //!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\n62: //!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\n63: //! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\n64: //!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Like [`Option`], its variants are\n65: //!   exported as well.\n66: //! * <code>[std::string]::{[String], [ToString]}</code>, heap-allocated strings.\n67: //! * <code>[std::vec]::[Vec]</code>, a growable, heap-allocated vector.\n68: //!\n69: //! Not controlled (user-defined) names should have higher priority than controlled names\n76:         //    built into the language or standard library. This way we can add new names into the\n77:         //    language or standard library without breaking user code.\n78:         // 2. \"Closed set\" below means new names cannot appear after the current resolution attempt.\n79:         // Places to search (in order of decreasing priority):\n80:         // (Type NS)\n81:         // 1. FIXME: Ribs (type parameters), there's no necessary infrastructure yet\n82:         //    (open set, not controlled).\n83:         // 2. They should be higher in priority than preludes\n98:         //    and probably even names in modules according to the \"general principles\" above. They\n99:         //    also should be subject to restricted shadowing because are effectively produced by\n100:         //    derives (you need to resolve the derive first to add helpers into scope), but they\n101:         //    should be available before the derive is expanded for compatibility.\n102:         //    It's mess in general, so we are being conservative for now.\n103:         // 1-3. `macro_rules` (open, not controlled), loop through `macro_rules` scopes. Have higher\n104:         //    priority than prelude macros, but create ambiguities with macros in modules.\n105:         // 1-3. Names in modules (both normal `mod`ules and blocks), loop through hygienic parents\n106:         //    (open, not controlled). Have higher priority than prelude macros, but create\n107:         //    ambiguities with `macro_rules`.\n108:         // 4. `macro_use` prelude (open, the open part is from macro expansions, not controlled).\n109:         // 4a. User-defined prelude from macro-use\n110:         //    (open, the open part is from macro expansions, not controlled).\n111:         // 4b. \"Standard library prelude\" part implemented through `macro-use` (closed, controlled).\n112:         // 4c. Edition-Based Macro_use Difference and Prelude Injection\\n\\nCompiler/rustc_builtin_macros/src/standard_library_imports.rs contains the `inject()` function that automatically adds prelude imports to every crate. Additionally, when edition >= 2018, the `ident_span` uses `span` rather than `call_site`, affecting macro hygiene.\\n\\nThe macro_use prelude is defined in compiler/rustc_resolve/src/lib.rs as `macro_use_prelude: FxIndexMap<Symbol, NameBinding<'ra>>`.
